Common systems: uranium-lead (half-life 4.47 Ga, for ancient rocks), potassium-argon (1.25 Ga, for volcanic rocks and the K-Pg boundary), carbon-14 (5,730 yr, for organic material under 50,000 yr). Multiple systems cross-check results. Isochron methods handle unknown initial daughter concentrations. The method has determined Earth's age at ~4.54 billion years.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q323 — Urban Planning
**What is the concept of '15-minute city' and what are its criticisms?**

The 15-minute city (Carlos Moreno, popularized by Anne Hidalgo's Paris) proposes that all essential daily needs — work, shopping, healthcare, education, parks, social venues — should be reachable within a 15-minute walk or bike ride from home. Goals: reduce car dependency, cut emissions, strengthen community, improve quality of life. Criticisms: (1) may entrench inequality — affluent neighborhoods already have dense amenities while poor areas don't, and retrofitting requires massive investment; (2) can feel coercive if enforced through access controls — Oxford traffic restrictions triggered real protests; (3) many jobs and specialized services cannot be distributed into every neighborhood; (4) may increase housing costs in well-served areas. Proponents argue it is a planning aspiration, not a physical restriction on movement.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q324 — Biochemistry
**How does enzyme inhibition work, and what is the difference between competitive and non-competitive inhibition?**

Enzymes bind substrates at their active site to catalyze reactions. Inhibitors reduce enzyme activity. In competitive inhibition, the inhibitor resembles the substrate and binds the active site directly; increasing substrate concentration overcomes it. Km (apparent) increases; Vmax unchanged. Example: statins competitively inhibit HMG-CoA reductase. In non-competitive inhibition, the inhibitor binds an allosteric site, changing enzyme shape so efficiency drops; increasing substrate does not help. Vmax decreases; Km unchanged. Example: cyanide inhibits cytochrome c oxidase. Uncompetitive inhibition requires substrate to bind first, then inhibitor binds the enzyme-substrate complex. Mixed inhibition combines features of both. These distinctions are critical in drug design: competitive inhibitors are more easily reversed.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q325 — Political Philosophy
**What is the difference between negative liberty and positive liberty?**

Isaiah Berlin (1958, 'Two Concepts of Liberty') distinguished: Negative liberty is freedom from external interference — the absence of coercion or obstacles imposed by other agents. A person is free to the extent that no one prevents them from acting. Associated with classical liberalism and libertarianism. Positive liberty is freedom to — the actual capacity to act and realize one's potential. A person is truly free only if they have real ability (resources, education, health) to pursue their goals. Associated with social democracy and welfare liberalism. Tension: Berlin worried that positive liberty could justify coercive paternalism — the state forcing people to be 'truly free' by overriding their apparent desires. Critics (Taylor, Sen) argue that negative liberty is also not purely formal: a poor person facing no coercion may still be profoundly unfree. The debate structures contemporary political divisions between libertarians and progressives.

**Rating: 10/10/10**

---

### Q326 — Linguistics
**What is the Sapir-Whorf hypothesis and how much does language shape thought?**

The Sapir-Whorf hypothesis proposes that language influences or determines thought. The strong version (linguistic determinism) — you cannot conceive what you lack words for — is largely rejected: people think about things they lack words for, translation works, and preverbal infants display complex reasoning. The weak version (linguistic relativity) has empirical support: (1) speakers of languages with many snow terms perceive snow distinctions more readily; (2) languages without left/right spatial terms (using absolute directions) shape spatial reasoning — Kuuk Thaayorre speakers maintain constant absolute orientation; (3) grammatical gender correlates with gendered mental associations; (4) color vocabulary affects cross-boundary color discrimination speed. Language shapes the ease and habitual patterns of thought, not what is thinkable in principle.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q327 — Astrophysics
**What is gravitational lensing and what has it revealed about the universe?**

Gravitational lensing occurs when massive objects bend light paths, as predicted by general relativity. Three regimes: (1) Strong lensing — produces visible arcs, Einstein rings, and multiple images; maps mass in galaxy clusters (Bullet Cluster evidence for dark matter). (2) Weak lensing — slight correlated distortions of background galaxy shapes; maps large-scale dark matter structure statistically. (3) Microlensing — temporary brightening when a compact object transits a background star; detects exoplanets and rogue planets. Discoveries: confirmation that dark matter outweighs visible matter 5:1 in clusters, detection of the most distant galaxies (magnified by foreground clusters), constraints on dark energy from cosmic structure growth, and hundreds of exoplanet detections.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q328 — Music Theory
**What makes a musical chord sound consonant or dissonant?**

Consonance and dissonance arise from acoustic and perceptual properties of frequency combinations. Intervals with simple integer frequency ratios (2:1 octave, 3:2 perfect fifth) produce fewer beating effects because their harmonics align; complex ratios produce more beating. Psychoacoustically, dissonance peaks when partials fall within a 'critical bandwidth' on the cochlea — roughly a minor second — causing roughness. Cultural context matters enormously: the tritone was 'diabolus in musica' in medieval polyphony but foundational in blues and jazz. In tonal music, dissonance creates tension that resolves to consonance, driving musical motion. Atonal composers (Schoenberg) emancipated dissonance from its obligation to resolve.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q329 — Immunology
**What is the difference between innate and adaptive immunity?**

Innate immunity is the fast, nonspecific first line of defense, responding within minutes to hours using pattern recognition receptors (toll-like receptors) that detect conserved microbial features (lipopolysaccharide, flagellin, viral RNA). Responses include phagocytosis by neutrophils and macrophages, inflammation, complement activation, and natural killer cell activity. No immunological memory is formed. Adaptive immunity is slow (days to weeks for first exposure), antigen-specific, and generates memory. B lymphocytes produce antibodies; cytotoxic CD8+ T cells kill infected cells; helper CD4+ T cells coordinate the response. Memory B and T cells persist after infection, enabling faster responses upon re-exposure — the basis of vaccination. Innate cells (dendritic cells) bridge both systems by presenting antigens to T cells and providing costimulatory activation signals.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q330 — Philosophy of Science
**What is the demarcation problem and how have philosophers attempted to solve it?**

The demarcation problem asks what distinguishes science from non-science or pseudoscience. Key attempts: (1) Verificationism (Vienna Circle) — meaningful only if empirically verifiable; fails because universal laws cannot be fully verified. (2) Falsificationism (Popper) — scientific if falsifiable, i.e., predictions could be proven wrong; astrology and Freudianism fail. Challenged by Duhem-Quine: anomalies can always be attributed to auxiliary hypotheses. (3) Kuhn — science is paradigm-governed puzzle-solving within a community, not defined by a logical criterion. (4) Lakatos — scientific research programs make novel predictions and generate progressive problem-shifts. (5) Thagard — pseudoscience fails to progress despite opportunity. No consensus criterion exists, suggesting demarcation is a family-resemblance problem rather than one with a sharp boundary.

**Rating: 10/10/10**

---

### Q331 — Economics
**What is the concept of 'moral hazard' and where does it appear?**

Moral hazard arises when one party takes greater risk because they don't bear the full consequences — another party absorbs the downside. Origins in insurance: insured parties may be less careful because losses are covered. Appearances: (1) Banking — 'too big to fail' banks take excessive risks knowing governments will bail them out (2008 crisis); (2) Health insurance — insured patients may overconsume healthcare; (3) Employment — employees may shirk if monitoring is imperfect (principal-agent problem); (4) Subprime lending — originators who sell loans have little incentive to screen borrowers. Solutions involve realigning incentives: deductibles, equity requirements, performance-based compensation, monitoring, and contractual penalties.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q332 — Neuroscience
**What is neuroplasticity and does the adult brain retain it?**

Neuroplasticity is the brain's ability to change structure and function in response to experience, learning, or injury. Forms: synaptic plasticity (LTP/LTD strengthening/weakening synapses), structural plasticity (growth/pruning of dendrites and axons), and neurogenesis (new neuron birth). Critical periods in development show peak plasticity; adult plasticity is reduced but real. Evidence: London taxi drivers have enlarged hippocampal posterior regions; musicians show expanded motor/auditory cortex for trained digits/tones; stroke recovery involves neighboring regions taking over lost functions; adult neurogenesis occurs in the hippocampal dentate gyrus and olfactory bulb. Plasticity is the mechanism underlying all learning and memory throughout life.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q333 — History of Technology
**How did the printing press change European society?**

Gutenberg's movable-type press (~1450) reduced book production cost ~99%, making texts widely accessible. Effects: (1) Literacy expansion and vernacular language promotion over Latin; (2) Protestant Reformation — Luther's theses spread across Germany in weeks; individuals could read scripture directly, undermining clerical authority; (3) Scientific Revolution — standardized texts enabled cumulative pan-European knowledge-building without handcopying errors; (4) Political change — pamphlets, propaganda, and newspapers democratized public discourse; (5) Information overload — contemporaries noted overwhelming 'sumptuousness of books,' requiring new selection skills. Eisenstein (1980) argues print created modernity's conditions: standardization, replication, and a shared information commons.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q334 — Chemistry
**What is chirality in chemistry and why does it matter biologically?**

A molecule is chiral if it is non-superimposable on its mirror image. The two mirror-image forms are enantiomers (R and S). Biology is built on chirality: amino acids are almost exclusively L-form; sugars are almost exclusively D-form. Enzymes and receptors are chiral and interact differently with enantiomers. Biological consequences: (1) Only L-amino acids are incorporated into proteins; D-forms are largely inactive or toxic; (2) Drug enantiomers differ dramatically — S-ibuprofen is the active painkiller; thalidomide's R enantiomer is sedative, S causes birth defects; (3) Taste/smell receptors distinguish enantiomers — (R)-limonene smells of oranges, (S)-limonene of lemons. Producing pure enantiomers via asymmetric synthesis is a major pharmaceutical challenge.

**Rating: 10/10/10**

---

### Q335 — Environmental Science
**What is the difference between the greenhouse effect and global warming?**

The greenhouse effect is a natural process essential for life: greenhouse gases (water vapor, CO2, methane, N2O) absorb infrared radiation emitted by Earth's surface and re-emit it back, trapping heat. Without it, Earth would average -18C instead of +15C. Global warming refers to the observed rise in Earth's average temperature since the mid-20th century, caused by the enhanced greenhouse effect — human emissions of CO2 (fossil fuels, deforestation), methane (agriculture, landfills), and N2O (fertilizers) increasing the atmosphere's heat-trapping capacity. The natural greenhouse effect is a prerequisite for habitable temperatures; the enhanced greenhouse effect disrupts climate systems that civilization developed under. 'Global warming' often encompasses climate change broadly: shifting precipitation, sea-level rise, extreme weather, and ecosystem disruption.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q336 — Psychology
**What is cognitive dissonance and how do people reduce it?**

Cognitive dissonance (Leon Festinger, 1957) is the discomfort from holding psychologically inconsistent cognitions. Classic demonstration: participants paid $1 (vs. $20) to tell others a boring task was interesting rated the task as more enjoyable — they had to justify their lie with insufficient external justification, convincing themselves the task wasn't boring. Reduction strategies: (1) Change one of the conflicting beliefs ('smoking isn't that harmful'); (2) Add consonant cognitions that outweigh the dissonance ('I exercise, so smoking is offset'); (3) Reduce the importance of the conflict; (4) Change behavior (quit smoking). People often prefer rationalization over behavior change. Dissonance drives post-decision rationalization, sunk-cost effects, self-justification, and attitude change after behavior.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q337 — Marine Biology
**How do deep-sea creatures survive extreme pressure and darkness?**

The deep sea features pressures up to 1,100 atm, near-freezing temperatures, and total darkness below 1,000m. Adaptations: (1) Pressure — unsaturated membrane lipids remain fluid under high pressure; proteins are stabilized for pressure; no gas-filled cavities; piezophilic bacteria have optimally adapted enzymes. (2) Darkness — 80%+ of deep-sea species produce bioluminescence for communication, predation, counterillumination camouflage, and mating; eyes may be huge and sensitive or absent. (3) Food scarcity — 'marine snow' of organic particles from above fuels food chains; chemosynthetic ecosystems at hydrothermal vents use hydrogen sulfide as energy source, independent of sunlight. (4) Cold — enzymes function at low temperatures through structural flexibility.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q338 — Ethics
**What is utilitarianism and what are its main objections?**

Utilitarianism (Bentham, Mill) holds that morally right actions maximize aggregate welfare, happiness, or preference satisfaction for the greatest number. It is consequentialist and impartial. Bentham's hedonic calculus quantified pleasure/pain; Mill distinguished higher and lower pleasures. Objections: (1) Aggregation — may justify harming a minority severely to benefit the majority; (2) Rights violations — may justify torture or killing one innocent to save five; (3) Demandingness — requires constant sacrifice for maximum aggregate good; (4) Measurement — how to compare subjective utility across persons; (5) Alienation — treats all relationships instrumentally. Rule utilitarianism and preference utilitarianism modify the theory but critics argue fundamental tensions remain.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q339 — Architecture
**What is the difference between load-bearing and curtain-wall construction?**

In load-bearing construction, walls carry structural weight — floors and roof rest on the walls. Walls must be thick; openings must be small; height is limited by wall thickness requirements. In curtain-wall (skeleton-frame) construction, an internal steel or reinforced concrete frame carries all loads; the exterior walls hang from the frame as a 'curtain' with no structural role. This enables vast glass facades, flexible open floor plans, much greater heights, and rapid construction. The Bessemer steel process and Otis elevator (1852) enabled the steel-frame skyscraper; the first is widely credited as Chicago's Home Insurance Building (1885).

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q340 — Mathematics
**What is the Riemann Hypothesis and why does it matter?**

The Riemann Hypothesis (1859) conjectures that all non-trivial zeros of the Riemann zeta function lie on the critical line where Re(s) = 1/2. The zeta function's zeros are intimately connected to prime number distribution — the Prime Number Theorem can be stated precisely using these zeros. If true, primes are distributed as regularly as possible; if false, unexpected clustering could occur. It is one of the seven Millennium Prize Problems ($1M prize). Verified computationally for the first 10^13 zeros, all on the critical line, but no proof exists. Hundreds of mathematical results are conditional on its truth, making it the most consequential unproven conjecture in mathematics.

**Rating: 10/10/10**

---

### Q341 — Anthropology
**What is the 'Anthropocene' and is it an official geological epoch?**

The Anthropocene is the proposed geological epoch in which human activity has become the dominant force shaping Earth's geology and ecosystems. Evidence: global radionuclide layer from 1950s nuclear testing; widespread plastic, concrete, and aluminum in the geological record; mass extinction rates 100-1000x background; CO2 and methane concentrations unprecedented in 800,000 years of ice cores; nitrogen cycle restructured by synthetic fertilizers. In 2023, the Anthropocene Working Group proposed formally designating it beginning ~1950, with Crawford Lake (Canada) as the boundary stratotype. In 2024, the International Union of Geological Sciences voted against formal designation, partly because the record is too recent and heterogeneous for a clear boundary. Status: scientifically compelling, geologically contested.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q342 — Physics
**What is the photoelectric effect and why did it require quantum mechanics to explain?**

The photoelectric effect: light striking metal ejects electrons only if frequency exceeds a threshold, regardless of intensity. Classical wave theory predicted any frequency should work given enough intensity. Instead: no electrons below threshold regardless of brightness; electron kinetic energy depends only on frequency not intensity; electrons ejected immediately. Einstein (1905) explained this by proposing light consists of discrete quanta (photons) with energy E = hf. Below threshold, photons lack energy to overcome the metal's work function. Higher frequency = higher-energy photons = higher-energy ejected electrons. Intensity only affects how many photons arrive (how many electrons ejected). This earned Einstein his only Nobel Prize (1921) and was pivotal evidence for quantum mechanics.

**Rating: 10/10/10**

---

### Q343 — Sociology
**What is Bourdieu's concept of 'cultural capital'?**

Bourdieu's cultural capital explains how social advantages reproduce across generations beyond direct economic inheritance. Three forms: (1) Embodied — dispositions, skills, and tastes acquired through socialization (speech patterns, cultural knowledge, institutional navigation skills); invisible but powerful; (2) Objectified — cultural goods (books, art) conferring status; (3) Institutionalized — formal credentials (degrees) officially recognizing cultural competence. Cultural capital converts to economic advantage in labor markets that privilege certain cultural codes. Key insight: what appears as individual merit or taste is largely class-based cultural transmission. Schools appear meritocratic but systematically reward students already socialized in dominant cultural codes — reproducing class inequality while legitimizing it as natural ability.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q344 — Medicine
**How does antibiotic resistance develop and spread?**

Antibiotic resistance develops through Darwinian evolution: antibiotics kill susceptible bacteria; resistant individuals survive and reproduce. Mechanisms: (1) enzymatic inactivation — beta-lactamases destroy penicillins; (2) efflux pumps expel antibiotics; (3) target modification — mutations alter binding sites (MRSA's modified penicillin-binding proteins); (4) reduced permeability prevents antibiotic entry. Spread: resistance genes on plasmids transfer horizontally between species via conjugation, dramatically accelerating spread. Key drivers: overprescription, agricultural use for growth promotion, incomplete treatment courses, healthcare spread. Multi-drug resistant organisms (MRSA, carbapenem-resistant Enterobacteriaceae) kill ~700,000 annually; projected 10 million by 2050 without intervention.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q345 — Philosophy of Mind
**What is the 'hard problem' of consciousness?**

David Chalmers (1995) distinguished the 'easy problems' of consciousness (explaining cognitive functions: attention, memory, behavior, reportability — tractable via neuroscience) from the 'hard problem': why any physical process gives rise to subjective experience — the felt quality of seeing red, tasting coffee, feeling pain (qualia). Even after fully explaining all functional processes, it seems a further question why this should feel like anything. Chalmers argues this is an explanatory gap suggesting either property dualism or panpsychism. Critics (Dennett) argue the hard problem is an illusion — explaining all the easy problems dissolves it. The debate remains unresolved and central to philosophy of mind.

**Rating: 10/10/10**

---

### Q346 — Cryptography
**What is public-key cryptography and how does RSA work?**

Public-key cryptography uses key pairs: a public key (shared openly) and a private key (kept secret). Anything encrypted with the public key can only be decrypted with the private key. RSA (Rivest, Shamir, Adleman, 1977): choose large primes p, q; compute modulus n=pq; compute phi(n)=(p-1)(q-1); choose public exponent e coprime to phi(n); compute private exponent d such that ed = 1 mod phi(n). Encrypt: C = M^e mod n; Decrypt: M = C^d mod n. Security rests on the integer factorization problem — factoring large n is computationally infeasible (2048+ bit keys). RSA is used for key exchange and digital signatures. Shor's quantum algorithm would break RSA, motivating post-quantum cryptography.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q347 — Art History
**What distinguishes Impressionism from Post-Impressionism?**

Impressionism (1860s-1880s; Monet, Renoir, Degas) captured fleeting light and atmosphere with loose brushwork, pure colors, and modern-life subjects en plein air. Post-Impressionism (~1886-1910) is an umbrella for artists who took Impressionism's freedom but pushed in different directions: Cezanne analyzed underlying geometric structure (influenced Cubism); Seurat developed Pointillism (pure color dots mixed optically); Gauguin pursued primitivism and symbolic color; Van Gogh used distorted line and expressive color for emotional intensity. What unites Post-Impressionists is dissatisfaction with the perceived superficiality of pure visual impression — they sought structure, symbol, or psychological depth.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q348 — Law
**What is the difference between civil law and criminal law?**

Criminal law: state prosecutes individuals for offenses against society; defendant faces punishment (imprisonment, fines). Standard of proof: beyond a reasonable doubt. Requires proof of both actus reus (guilty act) and mens rea (guilty mind). Civil law: disputes between private parties over rights and obligations (contracts, negligence, property, divorce); plaintiff seeks remedy (damages, injunction), not punishment. Standard of proof: balance of probabilities (preponderance). A person acquitted criminally can still be found civilly liable (O.J. Simpson — different standards). The same act can generate both criminal charges and civil liability simultaneously.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q349 — Ecology
**What is a keystone species and can you give examples?**

A keystone species has disproportionately large ecosystem effects relative to its abundance — removing it causes dramatic cascading changes (trophic cascade). Concept from Paine (1969): removing sea star Pisaster from intertidal zones allowed mussels to dominate, collapsing biodiversity. Examples: (1) Sea otters — eat sea urchins; without otters, urchins overgraze kelp forests, destroying habitat for hundreds of species; (2) Wolves in Yellowstone — reintroduced 1995; reduced elk overgrazing, allowing riparian vegetation recovery and riverbank stabilization; (3) African elephants — ecosystem engineers creating water holes, clearing brush, dispersing seeds; (4) Fig trees — year-round fruit during lean seasons supports entire frugivore communities. Keystone concept: not all species are interchangeable; losing certain ones triggers collapse.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q350 — Information Theory
**What is Shannon entropy and what does it measure?**

Shannon entropy H (Shannon 1948) measures uncertainty or information content: H(X) = -sum(p_i * log2(p_i)), in bits. Maximized when all outcomes are equally likely; zero when one outcome is certain. Interpretations: (1) average surprise when observing outcomes; (2) minimum average bits needed to encode messages from the source (source coding theorem); (3) irreducible uncertainty in the source. Examples: fair coin flip H=1 bit; fair 8-sided die H=3 bits; 99%-biased coin H~0.08 bits. Underlies data compression (optimal codes approach entropy), cryptography (key unpredictability), channel capacity (noisy-channel coding theorem), decision tree information gain, and statistical mechanics (Boltzmann entropy).

**Rating: 10/10/10**

---

### Q351 — Developmental Biology
**How does a single fertilized egg give rise to ~200 distinct cell types?**

All cells share the same genome but express different gene subsets — cell differentiation. Mechanisms: (1) Transcription factors — proteins binding DNA to activate/repress genes; cells inherit different profiles through lineage; (2) Epigenetics — DNA methylation and histone modifications change gene accessibility without altering sequence; (3) Cell signaling — morphogen concentration gradients from neighboring cells activate different transcription programs; (4) Asymmetric cell division — daughter cells inherit different cytoplasmic determinants; (5) Positive feedback loops — a fate decision reinforces itself (a neuron activates neuron-specific genes, repressing all others). These processes are coordinated by gene regulatory networks to reliably produce body plans from a single initial cell.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q352 — Computer Science
**What is the halting problem and why is it unsolvable?**

The halting problem asks: given any program and input, will it halt or run forever? Turing (1936) proved no algorithm can decide this for all cases. Proof by contradiction: assume decider H(P,I) exists. Construct D that runs H(P,P) and does the opposite: if H says P halts, D runs forever; if H says P loops, D halts. Running D on itself: D(D) halts iff H said 'loops' iff D runs forever — contradiction in both cases. No such H can exist. Consequences: there are fundamental limits to computability. Many practically important questions are undecidable: whether a program has a bug, whether two programs are equivalent. Turing established computability theory before computers existed.

**Rating: 10/10/10**

---

### Q353 — History
**What caused the fall of the Western Roman Empire?**

Multiple interacting causes: (1) Military overextension — reliance on Germanic foederati with questionable loyalty; long borders indefensible; (2) Economic decline — currency debasement caused inflation; agricultural decline; crushing taxation; (3) Political instability — ~50 emperors in 50 years during 3rd century Crisis; civil wars consumed resources; (4) External pressures — Hunnic invasion (~370 CE) pushed Goths across the Danube; Battle of Adrianople (378 CE) catastrophic defeat; (5) East-West split — resources concentrated in Byzantium; (6) Plague — Antonine and Cyprian plagues killed millions. Modern historians emphasize transformation: the Empire didn't simply 'fall' but transformed into medieval European kingdoms, preserving much Roman culture.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q354 — Nutrition
**What is the gut microbiome and how does it influence health?**

The gut microbiome is ~100 trillion microorganisms in the GI tract, encoding ~3 million genes. Functions: (1) Digestion — ferments fiber into short-chain fatty acids (butyrate, propionate, acetate) feeding colonocytes and reducing inflammation; synthesizes vitamins K and B12; (2) Immune education — early colonization trains the immune system; dysbiosis linked to autoimmune disease; (3) Gut-brain axis — vagus nerve, enteric nervous system, and microbial neurotransmitters (90% of body's serotonin produced in gut) link microbiome to mood and cognition; (4) Metabolic health — dysbiosis associated with obesity, T2 diabetes, cardiovascular disease. Key determinants: diet fiber diversity, birth mode, antibiotic exposure, breastfeeding.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q355 — Literature
**What makes a narrative an 'unreliable narrator' and what are famous examples?**

An unreliable narrator's account cannot be fully trusted due to limited knowledge, self-deception, mental instability, dishonesty, or bias (Booth coined the term, 1961). Types: (1) Naive — lacks understanding of their situation (Benjy in Faulkner's The Sound and the Fury); (2) Mentally unstable — Poe's Tell-Tale Heart narrator insists on sanity while revealing madness; (3) Self-deceiving — Stevens in Ishiguro's The Remains of the Day deceives himself about complicity in fascism; (4) Lying — Humbert Humbert in Lolita romanticizes his abuse of Dolores. Creates dramatic irony: reader perceives what narrator cannot. Explores self-deception, trauma, memory, and constructed identity.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q356 — Statistics
**What is the central limit theorem and why is it so important?**

The central limit theorem (CLT) states that the sampling distribution of the mean of i.i.d. random variables approaches a normal distribution as n increases, regardless of the underlying population distribution. If X1...Xn are i.i.d. with mean mu and finite variance sigma^2, then sqrt(n)(X_bar - mu)/sigma -> N(0,1) as n->inf. Practical convergence is often adequate at n>=30. Importance: (1) Justifies using normal-based tests (t-tests, z-tests, confidence intervals) on non-normal populations; (2) Explains why measurement errors are normally distributed (sum of many independent small influences); (3) Underpins polling and survey methodology; (4) Bridges probability theory and statistics. Without CLT, statistical inference would require knowing the exact population distribution.

**Rating: 10/10/10**

---

### Q357 — Astronomy
**Why does the Moon always show the same face to Earth?**

The Moon is tidally locked: its rotational period equals its orbital period (~27.3 days), so the same hemisphere always faces Earth. Mechanism: early in its history the Moon rotated faster. Earth's gravity created tidal bulges slightly ahead of the Earth-Moon line (due to faster rotation), which Earth's gravity pulled back on, exerting a decelerating torque. This slowed the Moon's rotation until it matched its orbital period — the stable tidal lock equilibrium. All large moons are eventually tidally locked to their planets. Earth is being slowly tidally locked to the Moon (day lengthening ~1.4 ms per century, Moon receding 3.8 cm/yr). Pluto and Charon are mutually tidally locked.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q358 — Economics
**What is Keynesian economics and how did it change economic policy?**

Keynes (1936) argued aggregate demand determines output and employment in the short run. In recessions, private spending falls; price rigidity prevents rapid adjustment; economy gets stuck in low-employment equilibrium. Paradox of thrift: rational individual saving collapses aggregate demand when universal. Government deficit spending provides fiscal stimulus; multiplier effect amplifies it through the economy. Policy implications: counter-cyclical fiscal policy (deficits in recessions, surpluses in booms); monetary policy insufficient at zero lower bound (liquidity trap); economy is not self-correcting in the short run. Applications: New Deal, post-WWII full employment policies, 2008-09 stimulus. Challenged by Friedman (crowding out, monetary rules) and Hayek (capital misallocation).

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q359 — Geology
**How do volcanoes form and why are they concentrated in specific regions?**

Volcanoes form where magma reaches the surface. Three settings: (1) Divergent boundaries — plates pull apart; mantle decompression melts rock; forms Mid-Atlantic Ridge, Iceland; (2) Subduction zones — oceanic plates dive beneath continental plates; water from subducting slab lowers melting point of mantle wedge; forms explosive stratovolcanoes in arcs (Andes, Cascades, Ring of Fire); (3) Hotspots — mantle plumes of anomalously hot rock; plate movement over fixed plume creates volcano chains (Hawaii, Yellowstone). 75% of active volcanoes lie in the Ring of Fire. Low-silica basaltic magma erupts effusively; high-silica silicic magma traps gas and erupts explosively.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q360 — Psychology
**What is the Dunning-Kruger effect and is the science behind it sound?**

The Dunning-Kruger effect (1999) describes a metacognitive bias where low-skill individuals overestimate their competence — they lack the knowledge to know what they don't know. High performers sometimes underestimate their abilities. Original study: bottom-quartile participants on reasoning/grammar tests rated themselves above average. Recent critiques: (1) The pattern may be a statistical artifact — regression to the mean and the better-than-average effect produce similar patterns from random data (Nuhfer 2016, Gignac & Zajenkowski 2020); (2) Low performers may be accurately uncertain, not specifically overconfident; (3) The effect is far smaller than popular culture suggests. The broader insight — ignorance can breed false confidence — has genuine basis, but the strong version is statistically contested.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q361 — Philosophy
**What is Occam's Razor and when should it be applied?**

Occam's Razor (William of Ockham, ~14th century): among competing hypotheses equally explaining the evidence, prefer the simplest — fewest assumptions. Applications: science prefers simpler theories until forced otherwise; statistics penalizes complexity (AIC, BIC); medicine prefers most common diagnosis. Caveats: (1) 'Simplest' is ambiguous — in parameters? assumptions? ontological commitments? (2) It is a heuristic, not a logical proof; the universe need not be simple; (3) Too much parsimony may discard real phenomena; (4) Einstein's version: 'as simple as possible, but not simpler.' The razor is a tie-breaker under equal explanatory power, not a license to dismiss complex explanations.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q362 — Neuroscience
**What happens in the brain during sleep and why do we need it?**

Sleep alternates NREM and REM cycles (~90 min). Functions: (1) Memory consolidation — slow-wave sleep (SWS): hippocampus replays experiences to neocortex for long-term storage; REM consolidates procedural and emotional memories; (2) Glymphatic clearance — CSF flowing through perivascular spaces flushes amyloid-beta and tau (Alzheimer's implicated proteins) predominantly during sleep; (3) Synaptic homeostasis (Tononi) — sleep downscales synaptic strength built up during waking, preventing saturation; (4) Immune function — sleep-promoting cytokines upregulate immune activity; (5) Energy restoration. Chronic deprivation accelerates cognitive decline, cardiovascular disease, diabetes, cancer. Adults need 7-9 hours; not reducible through habituation.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q363 — Economics
**What is a 'public good' in economics and what problem does it create?**

A public good is non-excludable (cannot prevent non-payers from using it) and non-rivalrous (one person's use doesn't reduce availability to others). Examples: national defense, clean air, basic research, public fireworks. Problem: free-rider problem — rational actors have no incentive to pay voluntarily since they benefit regardless. Private markets underprovide or fail to provide public goods even when total social benefit exceeds cost. Solution: government provision funded by mandatory taxation. Related concepts: club goods (excludable, non-rivalrous — streaming services); common pool resources (non-excludable, rivalrous — fisheries, tragedy of the commons). Public goods theory is central to justifying government market intervention.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q364 — Linguistics
**What is universal grammar and is it supported by evidence?**

Universal grammar (UG), Chomsky from 1960s: humans are born with an innate language acquisition device — universal structural principles constraining possible language forms. Evidence for: poverty of stimulus (children acquire complex structures without sufficient input for pure induction); critical periods; cross-linguistic universals (nouns/verbs, phrase structure, recursion); creolization (children spontaneously add complex grammar to pidgins). Evidence against: Piraha reportedly lacks recursion (Everett); cross-linguistic variation exceeds UG predictions; statistical learning accounts (Tomasello) show grammar can be extracted from input frequencies; usage-based approaches explain acquisition without innate structures. The nativist vs. constructivist debate remains active and unresolved.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q365 — Chemistry
**How does nuclear fission release energy and what makes it chain-reactive?**

Nuclear fission: a heavy nucleus (U-235 or Pu-239) absorbs a neutron and splits into two smaller nuclei plus 2-3 neutrons and ~200 MeV of energy. Energy source: products have less mass than reactants; mass deficit (delta m) converts to energy via E=mc^2; splitting heavy nuclei moves toward the binding-energy-per-nucleon peak at Fe-56. Chain reaction: the 2-3 released neutrons trigger further fissions exponentially. In bombs: uncontrolled supercritical chain reaction in microseconds. In reactors: moderators (water, graphite) slow neutrons; control rods (boron) absorb excess neutrons; maintain criticality k=1 (one fission triggers exactly one more). Critical mass: enough fissile material that neutron leakage doesn't prevent a self-sustaining chain reaction (~52 kg bare U-235 sphere).

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q366 — Cognitive Science
**What is working memory and what are its limits?**

Working memory temporarily holds and manipulates limited information for immediate use, enabling reasoning, learning, comprehension, and planning. Miller (1956): magical number 7+-2 chunks capacity. Baddeley-Hitch model (1974): (1) Central executive — attentional controller; (2) Phonological loop — verbal/auditory storage; (3) Visuospatial sketchpad — visual/spatial storage; (4) Episodic buffer (2000) — integrates across modalities and with long-term memory. True chunk capacity ~4 items when rehearsal is prevented (Cowan 2001). Capacity correlates with fluid intelligence and academic achievement. Explains why long instructions are forgotten, multitasking is costly, and reading comprehension fails when decoding demands are high.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q367 — Physics
**What is dark matter and what is the evidence for it?**

Dark matter is proposed non-luminous matter exerting gravitational effects without interacting electromagnetically. Evidence: (1) Galaxy rotation curves — outer stars orbit too fast for visible mass alone; flat curves require a massive unseen halo (Vera Rubin, 1970s); (2) Gravitational lensing — clusters bend light more than visible mass predicts; (3) Bullet Cluster — colliding galaxies show gas slowed by collision but gravitational mass (lensing maps) continuing ahead, confirming non-interacting dark matter; (4) CMB — temperature fluctuation pattern fits 27% dark matter, 5% ordinary matter, 68% dark energy; (5) Large-scale structure — galaxy filaments and voids require dark matter seeding. Leading candidates: WIMPs, axions. None directly detected.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q368 — History of Science
**How did the discovery of DNA's structure change biology?**

Watson, Crick, Franklin, and Wilkins determined the double-helix structure of DNA in 1953. Consequences: (1) Heredity mechanism — each strand templates its complement, explaining genetic transmission (the structure 'suggested its own copying mechanism'); (2) Gene-protein link — base sequence encodes protein sequence; central dogma DNA->RNA->protein; (3) Mutation mechanism — replication errors and chemical damage explain heritable variation; (4) Genetic engineering — restriction enzymes, PCR, gene cloning, CRISPR all follow from knowing structure; (5) Medicine — genetic disease causes identifiable; molecular pathways targetable. Opened molecular biology as a discipline; ultimately enabled genomics, synthetic biology, and personalized medicine.

**Rating: 10/10/10**

---

### Q369 — Ethics
**What is the trolley problem and what does it reveal about moral intuitions?**

Foot (1967) / Thomson (1985): divert trolley from 5 to 1? Most say yes. Footbridge variant: push a man off a bridge to stop the trolley, saving 5? Most say no — despite identical utilitarian math. Insights: (1) Doctrine of double effect — harm as means (pushing) vs. side effect (diversion) morally differs; (2) Personal force — directly causing harm feels morally different from allowing harm; (3) Agent-relative obligations — stronger duty to not directly harm than to prevent harm. Divergence challenges pure consequentialism and supports deontological intuitions. Neuroscience (Greene): personal-force variants activate emotional brain regions more, suggesting emotion drives the intuition — raises question of whether the intuition is reliable.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q370 — Materials Science
**What makes ceramics hard but brittle, and how is this being addressed?**

Ceramics are hard because ionic/covalent bonds resist plastic deformation; dislocations cannot move easily (charge constraints in ionic, directional bonds in covalent ceramics). Brittleness: no plastic flow to redistribute stress; cracks propagate unstably. Addressing brittleness: (1) Transformation toughening — stressed ZrO2 undergoes phase transformation that expands locally, closing crack tips; partially stabilized zirconia achieves ~8 MPa*sqrt(m) toughness; (2) Fiber reinforcement — ceramic matrix composites (CMC) with SiC fibers bridge cracks and deflect paths; used in jet engine hot sections; (3) Nanostructuring — grain boundaries impede crack propagation; (4) Laminate architectures mimicking nacre's brick-and-mortar structure combine hard layers with energy-absorbing interfaces.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q371 — Sociology
**What is Emile Durkheim's concept of anomie?**

Anomie (Durkheim, 1893/1897) describes normlessness — breakdown of social norms regulating individual behavior and desires. Durkheim argued human desires are limitless and must be regulated by society. When norms break down (rapid social change, economic crises), individuals lose sense of what to want or how to relate to others — causing anxiety, purposelessness, and anomic suicide. In Suicide (1897), Durkheim showed suicide rates varied with social integration and moral regulation, identifying four types: egoistic (low integration), altruistic (high integration), anomic (low regulation), fatalistic (high regulation). Anomie differs from Marx's alienation: it's about normative guidance failure, not material conditions or labor separation.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q372 — Biotechnology
**What is CRISPR-Cas9 and how does it edit genes?**

CRISPR-Cas9 is adapted from a bacterial immune system where CRISPR arrays store viral DNA fragments and Cas9 cuts matching viral DNA when reinfected. Repurposed for editing: (1) Design sgRNA complementary to target DNA sequence; (2) sgRNA directs Cas9 to that location; (3) Cas9 cuts both strands; (4) Cell repair: NHEJ introduces insertions/deletions (knockout), or HDR incorporates a template (precise editing). Applications: therapeutic gene correction (sickle cell disease — first approved CRISPR therapy 2023), agriculture, research. Extensions: base editing (no DSB), prime editing (search-and-replace), CRISPRi/a (regulate without cutting). Limitations: off-target cuts, delivery challenges, HDR inefficiency in non-dividing cells.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q373 — Economics
**What causes hyperinflation and what are historical examples?**

Hyperinflation (>50% monthly) occurs when money supply grows far faster than output — typically through deficit monetization (printing money). Self-reinforcing: anticipated inflation drives spending, increasing velocity, driving prices higher. Examples: (1) Weimar Germany (1921-23) — prices doubled every 3.7 days at peak; workers paid twice daily; (2) Zimbabwe (2007-09) — 89.7 sextillion percent annual inflation in Nov 2008; currency abandoned for USD; (3) Hungary (1945-46) — most severe ever; prices doubled every ~15 hours; (4) Venezuela (2016-present) — deficit monetization; ~1,000,000% annual rate in 2018. Resolution requires: credible new currency, balanced budget, often dollarization or external support.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q374 — Philosophy of Language
**What is the distinction between 'use' and 'mention' in language?**

Use: employing a word to refer to something in the world ('Paris is a city'). Mention: referring to the word itself as a linguistic object ("'Paris' has five letters"). Confusing them produces nonsense: 'Cat has three letters' is only true if 'cat' is mentioned, not used. Matters for: (1) Quotation and indirect speech — we mention words to report others' speech; (2) Metalinguistics — studying language requires mentioning linguistic units; (3) Tarski's theory of truth — distinguishing object language from metalanguage avoids the Liar Paradox ('This sentence is false' illegitimately uses a sentence that mentions itself); (4) Everyday prank — 'gullible is not in the dictionary' exploits the distinction.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q375 — Astronomy
**What is the cosmic microwave background and what does it tell us?**

The CMB is thermal radiation pervading the universe, discovered by Penzias and Wilson (1965). It is light from ~380,000 years after the Big Bang, when recombination allowed photons to travel freely for the first time (surface of last scattering), now redshifted to 2.725 K microwave radiation. Information: (1) Universe's age, composition (5% ordinary matter, 27% dark matter, 68% dark energy), and geometry (flat); (2) Temperature fluctuations (~1 part in 100,000) from acoustic oscillations in early plasma; (3) Fluctuation pattern provides initial conditions for all subsequent structure — galaxies form where CMB was slightly denser. The most precise blackbody spectrum in nature; primary evidence for the Big Bang.

**Rating: 10/10/10**

---

### Q376 — Psychology
**What is attachment theory and what are the main attachment styles?**

Bowlby's attachment theory: infants are biologically predisposed to bond with caregivers for survival; attachment system activates under threat (safe haven) and enables exploration (secure base). Ainsworth's Strange Situation identified styles: (1) Secure (~60%) — responsive caregiver; child explores confidently, comforted quickly on reunion; (2) Anxious-Ambivalent (~15%) — inconsistent caregiver; clingy, extremely distressed, not easily comforted; (3) Avoidant (~20%) — rejecting caregiver; child appears unaffected, avoids caregiver on reunion; (4) Disorganized (~5-10%) — frightening caregiver; contradictory behaviors; linked to trauma. Adult attachment styles (secure, anxious, avoidant, fearful-avoidant) influence romantic relationships, parenting, and stress responses.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q377 — Computer Science
**What is machine learning and how does it differ from traditional programming?**

Traditional programming: humans explicitly write rules determining behavior. Machine learning: system learns rules from data. Developer provides training data, model architecture, and objective function; optimization algorithm adjusts parameters to minimize error. Model learns statistical patterns rather than executing explicit rules. Types: supervised learning (labeled input-output pairs), unsupervised learning (structure in unlabeled data), reinforcement learning (learning from rewards). ML excels where rules are too complex to specify (image recognition, NLP, game playing) but produces opaque systems that can fail unexpectedly and reflect training data biases. Traditional programming excels where precise, auditable logic is required.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q378 — Anthropology
**What is cultural relativism and is it compatible with universal human rights?**

Cultural relativism (Boas, Benedict): practices should be understood within their own cultural context, not judged by external standards. Critique of ethnocentrism. Descriptive relativism (methodological): suspend prior judgments to understand other cultures. Normative relativism (moral): no cross-cultural standards; each culture's practices are equally valid. Tension with universal human rights: normative relativism would prevent condemning FGM, honor killings, or slavery. Most anthropologists accept descriptive but reject normative relativism. The challenge: articulating universal rights without imposing one culture's values — addressed through overlapping consensus (Rawls, Sen) or capabilities approach (Nussbaum).

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q379 — Medicine
**How do vaccines work and why do some require boosters?**

Vaccines train the adaptive immune system: (1) introduce antigen (killed/attenuated pathogen, protein subunit, or mRNA); (2) dendritic cells present fragments on MHC; (3) matching T and B lymphocytes activate; (4) B cells produce antibodies and memory B cells; (5) memory T cells persist. Re-exposure triggers rapid amplified response. Why boosters: (1) Antibody titers wane over time; (2) Pathogen mutation requires updated vaccines (flu, COVID variants); (3) Some vaccines provide strong but time-limited immunity (tetanus ~10 yr). Single-dose long-lived vaccines: MMR, yellow fever. mRNA vaccines (COVID-19) were first approved mRNA technology and are rapidly updatable.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q380 — Mathematics
**What is Godel's incompleteness theorem and why does it matter?**

Godel (1931): First theorem: any consistent formal system powerful enough to express basic arithmetic contains true statements unprovable within it. Proof constructs statement G ('G is not provable') — provable => inconsistent; unprovable => true but unprovable. Second theorem: no such system can prove its own consistency. Refuted Hilbert's program for a complete, consistent, finitely axiomatizable mathematics. Implications: (1) Mathematics is inherently incomplete — truths beyond any fixed proof system; (2) Certainty through formalization has limits; (3) Human mathematical intuition may transcend formal systems (Penrose); (4) Anticipates Turing's halting problem: self-referential statements about systems create undecidable propositions. Among the most profound results in intellectual history.

**Rating: 10/10/10**

---

### Q381 — Physics
**What is the difference between speed and velocity, and why does it matter?**

Speed is a scalar — magnitude only (60 km/h). Velocity is a vector — magnitude and direction (60 km/h due north). The distinction matters because: (1) Constant speed can coexist with changing velocity — a car rounding a curve at steady speed is accelerating (direction changes), requiring net force; (2) Displacement depends on velocity — a round trip has zero displacement regardless of speed; (3) Work and energy require vectors — a force perpendicular to velocity does no work; (4) Relative velocity is a vector sum — two cars at 100 km/h toward each other have 200 km/h relative velocity. Newton's second law F=ma involves acceleration (rate of change of velocity), so all classical mechanics is built on vectors. Conflating speed and velocity causes errors in kinematics, orbital mechanics, and electromagnetism.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q382 — Literature
**What is magical realism and how does it differ from fantasy?**

Magical realism (Garcia Marquez, Borges, Allende) presents supernatural elements as seamlessly integrated into a realistic world, without characters expressing surprise. In One Hundred Years of Solitude, Remedios the Beauty ascends to heaven while folding laundry — narrated with the same tone as household chores. Fantasy constructs a secondary world with its own supernatural rules (Tolkien) or signals the extraordinary as exceptional. Key distinctions: (1) Magical realism roots the supernatural in this world as cultural or psychological reality — expressing how communities experience time, grief, or history; (2) Fantasy provides internal logical consistency for its magic; (3) Magical realism carries political and postcolonial dimensions — the irrational reflects worldviews that European realism dismissed. Garcia Marquez described it as capturing how Latin Americans actually experience reality.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q383 — Biology
**What is convergent evolution and what are striking examples?**

Convergent evolution: unrelated organisms independently evolve similar traits in response to similar environmental pressures — same adaptive solution from different lineages. Examples: (1) Camera eye evolved independently in vertebrates, cephalopods (octopus), and box jellyfish; (2) Wings evolved independently in birds, bats, insects, and pterosaurs; (3) Streamlined bodies in dolphins (mammal), sharks (fish), and ichthyosaurs (reptile); (4) Echolocation evolved independently in bats and toothed whales; (5) Marsupial/placental pairs — thylacine and wolf had strikingly similar skull, dentition, and behaviour. Convergence is powerful evidence for natural selection: adaptive pressures produce predictable phenotypic outcomes even across vast evolutionary distances.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q384 — Economics
**What is the difference between GDP and GNP, and what does neither capture?**

GDP: total market value of final goods/services produced within a country's borders, regardless of producer. GNP/GNI: total produced by a country's residents, regardless of location. Difference: a US company's Mexican factory contributes to Mexico's GDP and US's GNP. Ireland's GDP >> GNI due to multinational headquarters. What neither captures: (1) Inequality — GDP per capita is a mean, not median welfare; (2) Unpaid work — childcare, housework add enormous value; (3) Environmental degradation — natural capital depletion invisible; (4) Leisure — shorter hours increase welfare but reduce GDP; (5) Quality of life — health, safety, happiness; (6) Informal economy. Alternatives: HDI, Genuine Progress Indicator, Bhutan's Gross National Happiness.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q385 — Neuroscience
**What is the default mode network and what does it do?**

The default mode network (DMN) — medial prefrontal cortex, posterior cingulate cortex, angular gyrus, medial temporal lobes — is more active during rest than during externally directed tasks. Discovered when these regions consistently deactivated during attention-demanding tasks (Raichle 2001). Functions: (1) Mind-wandering and spontaneous thought — daydreaming, rumination, future planning; (2) Self-referential processing — thinking about oneself; (3) Autobiographical memory retrieval; (4) Theory of mind — imagining others' mental states; (5) Future simulation. Disruptions: overactive DMN linked to depression/anxiety (excessive rumination); disorganised DMN linked to autism (reduced theory of mind); Alzheimer's preferentially attacks DMN regions. Psychedelics and meditation both substantially alter DMN activity, possibly explaining ego dissolution.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q386 — History
**What caused World War I and why did it escalate so rapidly?**

The assassination of Archduke Franz Ferdinand in Sarajevo (28 June 1914) was the immediate trigger, but underlying causes included: (1) Alliance systems — Triple Alliance (Germany, Austria-Hungary, Italy) vs Triple Entente (France, Russia, Britain); local conflict automatically drew in great powers; (2) Militarism — arms races, offensive doctrines (Schlieffen Plan assumed rapid French defeat before turning east); (3) Imperialism — colonial competition; (4) Nationalism — Pan-Slavism threatened Austria-Hungary's multi-ethnic empire. Rapid escalation: Austria ultimatum to Serbia → partial compliance → Austrian declaration of war → Russian mobilisation → German ultimatum → declarations cascade → Germany invaded Belgium → Britain declared war. Six weeks from regional dispute to continental war.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q387 — Philosophy
**What is the problem of other minds?**

The problem of other minds: how can I know that other people have conscious experiences rather than being philosophical zombies — behaviourally identical but with no inner experience? I have direct access only to my own conscious states and infer others' inner lives from behaviour, but this inference is not logically airtight. Responses: (1) Argument from analogy — my consciousness correlates with certain behaviours; I observe similar behaviours in others and infer similar consciousness; weak induction from one case; (2) Wittgenstein's private language argument — mental state concepts are inherently public/social; the grammar of mental language presupposes other minds; (3) Functionalism — mental states are functional roles; any system playing those roles has them; (4) Embodied/enactivist — consciousness is expressed in behaviour, not hidden behind it. Directly bears on animal welfare, AI consciousness, and moral status of other beings.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q388 — Computer Science
**What is the difference between a process and a thread?**

A process is an independent program in execution with its own memory address space, file handles, and OS resources; processes are isolated from each other. A thread is a unit of execution within a process — multiple threads share the same address space and heap but each has its own stack and registers. Key differences: (1) Isolation — crashed thread can corrupt all threads in process; crashed process cannot corrupt others; (2) Creation cost — spawning a process is expensive; threads are cheap; (3) Communication — threads share memory directly (fast, needs synchronisation); processes use IPC (slower, safer); (4) Parallelism — threads can run truly parallel on multi-core CPUs; Python's CPython GIL prevents true parallel Python thread execution, making multiprocessing preferred for CPU-bound tasks.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q389 — Astronomy
**What is a neutron star and what makes it so extreme?**

A neutron star is the collapsed remnant of a massive star after core-collapse supernova. Electron-proton fusion forms neutrons; neutron degeneracy pressure halts further collapse. Extremes: radius ~10-12 km; mass 1.4-2.0 solar masses; density ~10^17 kg/m^3 (teaspoon = 100 million tonnes = atomic nucleus density); surface gravity 2×10^11 m/s^2; escape velocity ~half light speed; magnetic fields up to 10^15 Gauss (magnetars); millisecond pulsars spin hundreds of times/sec (used as gravitational wave detectors). Binary neutron star mergers produce gravitational waves (LIGO 2017) and are primary source of heavy r-process elements (gold, platinum).

**Rating: 10/10/10**

---

### Q390 — Sociology
**What is the sociological concept of 'social capital'?**

Social capital (Bourdieu, Coleman, Putnam) — resources embedded in social relationships: trust, norms, networks enabling coordination. Three definitions: Bourdieu — capital from durable network of relationships, unequally distributed, convertible to economic advantage; Coleman — public good from relationships reducing transaction costs; Putnam — bonding capital (strong ties within homogeneous groups) vs bridging capital (weak ties across diverse groups). Bridging capital correlates with economic development, democratic health, social mobility. High-trust societies have lower transaction costs, faster growth, better government. Putnam's Bowling Alone (2000) documented US civic participation decline. Dark side: tight bonding within exclusionary groups (gangs, cults) can harm outsiders.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q391 — Chemistry
**What is pH, and what does it actually measure?**

pH = -log10[H+], the concentration of hydrogen ions (hydronium H3O+) in mol/L. Scale 0-14 in practice; pH 7 neutral (pure water at 25C), below 7 acidic, above 7 basic. Logarithmic: each unit = 10x change in [H+]; pH 3 is 100x more acidic than pH 5. Henderson-Hasselbalch equation: pH = pKa + log([A-]/[HA]) for buffers. Biological significance: enzymes function within narrow pH ranges (pepsin pH 1-2; blood pH 7.35-7.45; deviation of 0.2 units causes acidosis/alkalosis). pH affects solubility, reaction rates, protein folding, membrane function. Introduced by Soren Sorensen (1909).

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q392 — Psychology
**What is confirmation bias and how does it affect reasoning?**

Confirmation bias: tendency to seek, interpret, and remember information that confirms pre-existing beliefs while discounting contradictory evidence. Wason's selection task (1960) showed people test rules by seeking confirmation rather than falsification. Manifestations: (1) Selective search for confirming evidence; (2) Ambiguous evidence interpreted as confirming; (3) Confirming memories better retained; (4) Attitude polarisation — same mixed evidence makes both sides more convinced of their opposing positions. Consequences: sustains false beliefs, fuels political polarisation, causes medical diagnosis errors, financial mistakes, wrongful convictions. Debiasing: consider the opposite, pre-mortem, adversarial collaboration, devil's advocate. Arguably the most pervasive and damaging cognitive bias.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q393 — Ecology
**What is the difference between a food chain and a food web?**

Food chain: linear sequence where each organism is eaten by the next (phytoplankton -> zooplankton -> small fish -> shark). Food web: realistic network of all food chains in an ecosystem — most organisms have multiple prey and predators. Why food webs matter: (1) Resilience — multiple food sources buffer against species loss; (2) Trophic cascades — removing top predator ripples through entire web (Yellowstone wolves); (3) 10% energy transfer rule — only 10% of energy passes to next trophic level, so a 4-level chain captures 0.1% of original energy at top; (4) Bioaccumulation — toxins (DDT, mercury) concentrate at higher trophic levels as each organism retains toxin from all prey.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q394 — Mathematics
**What is a derivative and what does it actually represent?**

A derivative is the instantaneous rate of change of a function: f'(x) = lim[h->0] (f(x+h)-f(x))/h. Geometrically: slope of the tangent line at a point. Physically: if position is f(t), derivative is velocity; second derivative is acceleration. Applications: (1) Optimisation — f'(x)=0 at maxima/minima; (2) Physics — Maxwell's equations, quantum mechanics; (3) Economics — marginal cost/revenue; (4) Biology — population growth rate; (5) Machine learning — gradient descent follows negative derivative of loss function. Fundamental theorem of calculus: differentiation and integration are inverse operations.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q395 — Medicine
**What is the blood-brain barrier and why does it make treating brain diseases difficult?**

The BBB is a highly selective permeability barrier formed by specialised brain capillary endothelial cells with tight junctions (occludin, claudins), reinforced by astrocyte end-feet and pericytes. Unlike elsewhere, paracellular movement is blocked. What crosses: small lipid-soluble molecules (O2, CO2, ethanol, anaesthetics), glucose (GLUT1), amino acids. What doesn't: ~98% of small-molecule drugs, all antibodies, large proteins, most bacteria. Treatment difficulty: brain tumours, infections, Alzheimer's, and neurological disorders require drugs that can't penetrate. BBB paradoxically breaks down in inflammation. Workarounds: lipophilic prodrugs, focused ultrasound (temporarily opens junctions), nanoparticle carriers, intranasal delivery, intrathecal injection.

**Rating: 9/10/10**

---

### Q0 — Test
**Test question?**

Test answer.

**Rating: 9.0/10**

---

### Q396 — Linguistics
**What is the Sapir-Whorf hypothesis and how strong is the evidence for it?**

The strong version (linguistic determinism): language determines thought. Mostly rejected.

The weak version (linguistic relativity): language *influences* thought. Real evidence:

**Colour terms**: Russian has goluboy/siniy (light/dark blue). Russian speakers distinguish shades faster near that boundary.

**Spatial reasoning**: Guugu Yimithirr and Kuuk Thaayorre use absolute cardinal directions (N/S/E/W), not left/right. Speakers outperform English speakers on dead-reckoning.

**Number**: Pirahã has only 'few' and 'many.' Speakers struggle with exact quantity tasks.

**Counterfactuals**: Languages with clear counterfactual grammar affect causal reasoning tasks (Au 1983).

**Conclusion**: you're not trapped by your language — translation works, new concepts can be learned. But lexicalisation makes patterns more salient and automatic. Effect is real but modest.

**Rating: 8.5/10**

---

### Q397 — History
**What caused the collapse of the Bronze Age around 1200 BCE?**

One of history's great mysteries: nearly every eastern Mediterranean civilisation collapsed within ~50 years. Mycenaean Greece, Hittite Empire, Ugarit, Canaan, Egyptian New Kingdom weakened.

**Proposed causes:**
1. **Sea Peoples invasions** — Egyptian records show waves of migrants. Likely a symptom, not cause.
2. **Drought/climate change** — Pollen studies and grain records from Ugarit show multi-decade drought from ~1200 BCE. Starvation, state failure, migration, raids.
3. **Earthquakes** — Destruction layers at Mycenae, Tiryns. But not all sites.
4. **Systems collapse** (Eric Cline, 1177 B.C.): Bronze Age trade was deeply interdependent — tin from Afghanistan, copper from Cyprus, grain from Egypt. One shock cascaded.
5. **Internal revolts** — Linear B tablets at Pylos show defensive preparations and reduced rations before destruction.

**Consensus**: multicausal. Drought stressed food, weakened states, Sea Peoples applied the final blow. Interconnectedness amplified fragility.

**Rating: 9.0/10**

---

### Q398 — Computer Science
**What is a hash function and why does cryptographic hashing matter?**

A hash function maps arbitrary-length input to fixed-length output.

**Cryptographic hash** requires:
1. **Preimage resistance**: given H(x), can't find x.
2. **Second preimage resistance**: given x, can't find x' where H(x')=H(x).
3. **Collision resistance**: can't find any x, x' where H(x)=H(x').
4. **Avalanche effect**: one bit change flips ~50% of output bits.

**Why it matters:**
- Passwords: store H(password), not password. Bcrypt/Argon2 add deliberate slowness.
- Digital signatures: sign H(document) — if hash is unforgeable, signature is too.
- Integrity: SHA-256 confirms file wasn't tampered with.
- Blockchain: each block contains H(previous block) — tamper-evident chain.

**Broken**: MD5 and SHA-1 have known collisions. Use SHA-256 or SHA-3.

**Rating: 9.0/10**

---

### Q399 — Philosophy
**What is the problem of induction, and has it been solved?**

David Hume (1739): we observe the sun rising every day and conclude it will rise tomorrow. But what justifies that inference? Past regularities don't logically entail future ones.

The problem: induction can't be justified by logic (that's deduction) or by past success (circular).

**Attempted solutions:**
1. **Pragmatic** (Reichenbach): if nature is uniform, induction works; if not, no method works. Nothing lost by trying.
2. **Falsificationism** (Popper): eliminate induction. Science = bold conjectures + deductive attempts to falsify. Problem: doesn't explain why to trust unfalsified theories.
3. **Bayesian**: induction is probabilistic updating. Rational degrees of belief, not certainty. Problem: priors need justification.
4. **Dissolution** (Wittgenstein): 'justified belief' just means what it means in practice.

**Consensus**: not solved. Popper dominant in philosophy of science; Bayesian inference dominates practice. Hume's raw problem remains open.

**Rating: 9.0/10**

---

### Q400 — Biology
**How does CRISPR-Cas9 actually work at the molecular level?**

CRISPR is a bacterial immune system repurposed as a gene editor.

**Mechanism:**
1. **Guide RNA (gRNA)**: 20-nucleotide sequence matching target DNA, binds to Cas9.
2. **PAM sequence**: Cas9 requires NGG adjacent to target (SpCas9). Without PAM, no cut.
3. **Scanning**: gRNA-Cas9 complex scans DNA for complementary sequence next to PAM.
4. **Unwinding**: Cas9 unwinds double helix locally for strand invasion.
5. **Cleavage**: Two nuclease domains (RuvC + HNH) each cut one strand — blunt double-strand break.

**Repair pathways:**
- **NHEJ**: fast, error-prone; creates indels, disrupts gene. Used to knock out genes.
- **HDR**: if template supplied, copies it in for precise editing. Less efficient.

**Applications**: sickle cell (cured in trials), cancer immunotherapy, crop improvement, diagnostics (SHERLOCK/DETECTR).

**Safety**: off-target cuts remain a concern. Base editors and prime editors reduce this.

**Rating: 9.5/10**

---

### Q401 — Physics
**What is quantum entanglement and does it allow faster-than-light communication?**

Two particles share a quantum state such that measuring one instantly determines the other, regardless of distance.

**Mechanism**: two photons in superposition |up-down> + |down-up>. Neither is definite. Measure one as spin-up, the other instantly becomes spin-down. Einstein called this 'spooky action at a distance.'

**Bell's theorem** (1964): local hidden variable theories can't explain quantum correlations. Bell inequality violations confirmed experimentally (Aspect 1982; loophole-free Hensen 2015) — correlations are genuinely non-local.

**But no FTL communication** (no-communication theorem): you can't control which outcome you get — results are random. Correlations only appear when you compare results via classical communication (limited to c).

**Applications**: quantum computing (entanglement enables speedup), quantum cryptography (BB84 — eavesdropping disturbs entanglement, detectable), quantum teleportation (transfers quantum state, not matter, still needs classical channel).

**Rating: 9.5/10**

---

### Q402 — Economics
**What is comparative advantage and why does it justify trade even when one country is better at everything?**

Ricardo (1817): even if one country is absolutely better at everything, both benefit from specialising based on *relative* efficiency.

**Example**: England (100h cloth, 120h wine) vs Portugal (90h cloth, 80h wine). Portugal better at both. But opportunity cost: Portugal gives up 0.89 cloth per wine; England gives up 1.2. Portugal has comparative advantage in wine, England in cloth.

**Mechanism**: opportunity cost, not absolute productivity, determines gains from trade. Both countries can consume more of both goods by specialising.

**Complications**: assumes full employment (fails short-term); ignores adjustment costs; dynamic effects (infant industry argument); doesn't distribute gains equally — trade creates winners and losers within countries.

**Still valid**: explains why nearly all economists support free trade. Political problem is distributional, not efficiency-based.

**Rating: 9.0/10**

---

### Q403 — Neuroscience
**What happens in the brain during sleep, and why do we need it?**

Sleep is not passive. The brain cycles through NREM and REM in ~90-minute cycles.

**Stages**: NREM1 (drowsy), NREM2 (sleep spindles, K-complexes — motor memory), NREM3/slow-wave (delta waves, growth hormone, declarative memory consolidation), REM (brain active as waking, body paralysed — dreams, emotional memory).

**Why we need it:**
1. Memory consolidation: hippocampus replays experiences, transfers to cortex during slow-wave (Stickgold 2005)
2. Glymphatic clearance: CSF flushes beta-amyloid and tau during sleep (Xie 2013) — disrupted in Alzheimer's
3. Synaptic homeostasis (Tononi): sleep downscales synapses ~20%, maintaining signal-to-noise ratio
4. Immune function: cytokine production, T-cell activation
5. Metabolic restoration: glucose regulation, ATP

**Sleep deprivation**: 17-19h awake impairs like 0.05% BAC. 24h like 0.10%. Fatal familial insomnia: prion destroys sleep circuits, death in months.

**Rating: 9.5/10**

---

### Q404 — Mathematics
**What is the Riemann Hypothesis and why do mathematicians care so much about it?**

Riemann zeta function: z(s) = 1 + 1/2^s + 1/3^s + ... extended to all complex numbers.

Riemann (1859): trivial zeros at s = -2, -4, -6...; infinitely many non-trivial zeros in critical strip 0 < Re(s) < 1.

**Hypothesis**: all non-trivial zeros lie on Re(s) = 1/2.

**Why it matters:**
- Prime number distribution: zeros control error in Prime Number Theorem. RH true => tightest possible bound O(sqrt(x) log x)
- 1000+ theorems conditional on RH
- Cryptography: prime distribution affects RSA security
- Physics: zero spacing matches eigenvalues of random Hermitian matrices (GUE) — mysterious connection to quantum chaos

**Status**: verified for first 10^13 zeros; no counterexample; no proof. Clay Millennium Problem ($1M prize). One of hardest open problems in mathematics.

**Rating: 10.0/10**

---

### Q405 — Sociology
**What is social capital, and does it actually predict outcomes in the real world?**

Social capital (Bourdieu, Coleman, Putnam): value embedded in social networks — trust, reciprocity norms, resource access through relationships.

**Three types (Putnam):**
- Bonding: within tight groups (family). Good for hard times; can exclude outsiders.
- Bridging: across groups. More economically valuable — weak ties (Granovetter 1973) are how most people find jobs.
- Linking: ties to institutions and authority.

**Evidence:**
- Health: Berkman & Syme 1979 — social isolation as deadly as 15 cigarettes/day.
- Crime: Sampson — neighbourhood collective efficacy predicts crime even controlling for poverty.
- Economic growth: Knack & Keefer 1997 — trust in strangers correlates with GDP growth.
- Italy: Putnam — regional governance quality mapped to medieval civic associational life.

**Critique**: causation hard to establish; reinforces inequality (who has high-value networks?); Bourdieu frames it as class reproduction mechanism.

**Rating: 9.0/10**

---

### Q406 — Chemistry
**What is entropy and why does it always increase?**

Entropy (S) measures the number of microscopic arrangements (microstates) producing the same macroscopic state. Boltzmann: S = k*ln(W).

**Why it increases**: pure statistics. Vastly more disordered arrangements exist than ordered ones. Gas expanding into vacuum isn't forced — it's just that 10^23 molecules spontaneously re-compressing is so improbable it never happens on cosmological timescales.

**Second Law**: in an isolated system, entropy never decreases. Total entropy of the universe increases in any irreversible process.

**Clarifications:**
- Local entropy CAN decrease (refrigerator, crystal, life) — but only by increasing entropy elsewhere by more
- Earth takes in low-entropy sunlight, radiates high-entropy heat
- Time's arrow: Second Law is the only fundamental physics law distinguishing past from future

**Maxwell's demon** (Landauer 1961): demon's memory erasure costs at least k*T*ln(2) per bit — net entropy still increases. Information and thermodynamics deeply linked.

**Rating: 9.5/10**

---

### Q407 — Psychology
**What is cognitive dissonance and how do people actually resolve it?**

Festinger (1957): holding two conflicting cognitions creates psychological discomfort. Three resolution strategies:

1. **Change a belief**: smoker convinces themselves they're immune.
2. **Change behaviour**: actually quit. Hardest.
3. **Add cognitions**: 'smoking reduces stress, which also causes cancer — net neutral.'

**Classic experiment** (Festinger & Carlsmith 1959): paid $1 or $20 to lie a boring task was interesting. $1 group later genuinely rated task as interesting — they had to justify compliance, so changed attitude. *Behaviour precedes belief when external justification is low.*

**Post-purchase rationalisation**: after buying, people seek confirming info and dismiss negatives.

**Belief perseverance** (When Prophecy Fails — doomsday cult): when predicted disaster didn't occur, members became *more* fervent, not less. Dissonance resolved by increasing commitment.

Dissonance magnitude: greater when decisions are important, irreversible, options similar in quality.

**Rating: 9.0/10**

---

### Q408 — Astronomy
**How do astronomers know the age of the universe is 13.8 billion years?**

Multiple independent methods converge:

1. **CMB (Planck)**: temperature fluctuation power spectrum fit to LCDM model gives 13.787 +/- 0.020 Gyr
2. **Hubble constant + expansion history**: H0 combined with Friedmann equations gives look-back time. Problem: H0 tension (local ~73 vs CMB ~67.4 km/s/Mpc) — unresolved
3. **Oldest stars**: globular clusters; HD 140283 'Methuselah star' = 14.46 +/- 0.8 Gyr — consistent within error
4. **Radioactive dating (cosmochronometry)**: U/Th ratios in old stars give ~12-14 Gyr, independent of expansion models

**Why trust it**: four independent methods (CMB, expansion history, stellar ages, nuclear physics) all point to 13-14 Gyr. Concordance is extraordinary.

**Rating: 9.5/10**

---

### Q409 — Law
**What is the difference between civil law and common law legal systems?**

**Common law** (UK, US, Australia, Canada, India):
- Judge-made law; past decisions (stare decisis) bind future courts
- Law develops case-by-case
- Adversarial: two parties argue before neutral judge
- Juries common in criminal and civil cases

**Civil law** (France, Germany, Europe, Latin America, Japan):
- Comprehensive codes from Roman law (Justinian's Corpus Juris Civilis)
- Statutes primary; judges interpret, don't create
- Inquisitorial: judge actively investigates
- Less precedent; fewer juries

**Key practical differences:**
- Discovery: US allows extensive pre-trial evidence exchange; civil law more limited
- Contract interpretation: common law literal text; civil law good faith and purpose
- Torts: common law case-by-case; civil law general clauses (French Art. 1382)

**Hybrid**: Louisiana, Quebec, Scotland — civil law base with common law influence.

**Rating: 8.5/10**

---

### Q410 — Technology
**What is Moore's Law and is it still true?**

Gordon Moore (1965): transistors on a chip double every ~2 years at same cost. Not physical law — empirical observation, self-fulfilling prophecy shaping semiconductor roadmaps.

**Historical accuracy**: held ~1965-2015. From 4 transistors (1959) to 10B+ (modern).

**What changed:**
